  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_9ec2a1a3-8550-47f8-a8bb-959eeeb3f55f'
      });
    }

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2626236022" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer284094225680287941">If you're into stuff like this, <a href='https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1991/05/kublai-khan-wasteland-prufrock-and.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>you can read the full review.</a><br/><br/><br/><br/>Kublai Khan: "The Wasteland, Prufrock and Other Poems" by T. S. Eliot<br/><br/><br/><br/><br/>(Original review, 1981-05-10)<br/><br/><br/>It seems to me that the author of 'Prufrock' and that of the Wasteland are so different as to be un-recognisable. A look at the Wasteland reveals a lot of, to me, gratuitous classical referencing for which we might like to blame Pound and while I value its novelty (whereas Prufrock reads like Kublai Khan) the Wasteland reads like deliberat</span>
  <span id="freeText284094225680287941" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/1991/05/kublai-khan-wasteland-prufrock-and.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br /><br />Kublai Khan: "The Wasteland, Prufrock and Other Poems" by T. S. Eliot<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />(Original review, 1981-05-10)<br /><br /><br />It seems to me that the author of 'Prufrock' and that of the Wasteland are so different as to be un-recognisable. A look at the Wasteland reveals a lot of, to me, gratuitous classical referencing for which we might like to blame Pound and while I value its novelty (whereas Prufrock reads like Kublai Khan) the Wasteland reads like deliberate pastiche.</span>

<div id="review_31487396" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31487396" />
    <a title="OldPoltroon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1469789-oldpoltroon"><img alt="OldPoltroon" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31487396?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="OldPoltroon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="OldPoltroon" href="/user/show/1469789-oldpoltroon">OldPoltroon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31487396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer660959509426592476">Uncle! <br/><br/>I measured out my life with coffee spoons in the hours, weeks, indeed months between when I first picked this up and when I subsequently set it down unfinished. It gets two stars in deference to the world of literary critics and english PhD's who call Eliot a master. <br/><br/>I want to believe that good poetry has something to share with us. I even keep a copy of Garrison Keillor's anthology "Good Poem's For Hard Times" on my night stand, for Pete's sakes! It's there right now, see? (Ok maybe ther</span>
  <span id="freeText660959509426592476" style="display:none">Uncle! <br /><br />I measured out my life with coffee spoons in the hours, weeks, indeed months between when I first picked this up and when I subsequently set it down unfinished. It gets two stars in deference to the world of literary critics and english PhD's who call Eliot a master. <br /><br />I want to believe that good poetry has something to share with us. I even keep a copy of Garrison Keillor's anthology "Good Poem's For Hard Times" on my night stand, for Pete's sakes! It's there right now, see? (Ok maybe there the tv remote is sitting on top of it). But, some of those poems I understand - I think.<br /><br />Ole' T.S. is smarter than me and I think that's the problem here. I could get through a Lovesong for J. Alfred Prufrock well enough, but so much of The Wasteland is unintelligable to me that I found myself reading a page and setting this down. It's disgraceful - the thing can't be 75 pages and here I sit, unable to get through it. <br /><br />I think the last time I understood poetry was in fifth grade when I had to recite a passage from "The Midnight Ride of Paul Revere." Since then I've found that most poetry completely and utterly befuddles me.<br /><br />Well, I'm dumb. I admit it. <br /><br />Uncle.<br /><br />-m</span>
  <a data-text-id="660959509426592476" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14351078?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/580941-jason">Jason</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14351078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5898102432389503078">T.S. Eliot takes a lot of work. I wouldn't recommend just plowing through The Wasteland on your own. It's the type of poem you only really understand when you discuss it in a group. If I hadn't studied it in a class in college, I'm sure I never would've understood it.<br/><br/>I would give 5 stars to Prufrock alone, and probably 3 or 4 to the rest. I especially loved Prufrock when I was single, b/c I think it captures the essence of male timidity. The language is oblique, but has some powerful contrasting</span>
  <span id="freeText5898102432389503078" style="display:none">T.S. Eliot takes a lot of work. I wouldn't recommend just plowing through The Wasteland on your own. It's the type of poem you only really understand when you discuss it in a group. If I hadn't studied it in a class in college, I'm sure I never would've understood it.<br /><br />I would give 5 stars to Prufrock alone, and probably 3 or 4 to the rest. I especially loved Prufrock when I was single, b/c I think it captures the essence of male timidity. The language is oblique, but has some powerful contrasting imagery. And it boasts probably the best opening lines of all time: "Let us go now you and I, and watch the evening spread against the sky, like a patient etherized upon a table." That's the gist of it anyway. Never a more jarring simile than this.<br /><br />I recommend Prufrock if you're up for a little challenge, and the Wasteland only if you're really ambitious.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5898102432389503078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_148908597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/148908597" />
    <a title="Ashley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4958601-ashley"><img alt="Ashley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548257726p2/4958601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/148908597?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley" href="/user/show/4958601-ashley">Ashley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486400617" href="/book/show/436605.The_Waste_Land_Prufrock_and_Other_Poems">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4958601-ashley?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer148908597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6088922690059770859">If you asked me a few years ago to make a list of who I thought would be on the list of poets most influential to my own voice, I would never have selected Eliot. I have always been a little intimidated by his intelligence. For me, the key to gaining a deeper appreciation for Eliot was a deeper study of Anglo-Saxon poetry. Modernists like Eliot and Auden and Pound most remind me, for all their experimentation, of those old tellers of epic tales: the attention to language and rhythm, the idea of </span>
  <span id="freeText6088922690059770859" style="display:none">If you asked me a few years ago to make a list of who I thought would be on the list of poets most influential to my own voice, I would never have selected Eliot. I have always been a little intimidated by his intelligence. For me, the key to gaining a deeper appreciation for Eliot was a deeper study of Anglo-Saxon poetry. Modernists like Eliot and Auden and Pound most remind me, for all their experimentation, of those old tellers of epic tales: the attention to language and rhythm, the idea of poetic authority; even hints of what would become Eliot's theory of objective correlative are hinted at in Old English works. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6088922690059770859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6727276592565070287" style="display:none">I studied this during my A Level English Literature class. I remember the long, long, drawn out agony of the endless reading aloud of this poetry in my English Literature classes, and the feelings of utter and total crazed despair, frustration and boredom that made me want to stand up and scream "no more!!!!" I'm know this poetry is an accomplished literary masterpiece, but I could not understand anything or relate to anything TS Eliot wrote, though I wish it were not so. The one star merely reflects my limited understanding and the fact that I sadly got nothing at all from this poetry, which is blighted and biased, I know, by the nightmarish memories of an 18 year old self studying this.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_623960046" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/623960046" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5429379-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369448712p2/5429379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/623960046?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/5429379-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451526847" href="/book/show/118391.The_Waste_Land_and_Other_Poems">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer623960046&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer623960046" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6194810520046338597">T.S. Eliot’s poem The Waste Land is a collection of fragments. There are 5 parts to the poem and the connection between these parts are noted to be anything but obvious, yet they all hold different layers of voices and obscure elements to the non-traditional reader. The poem as a whole constantly shifts its point of view and is abundantly full of references of classic literature from varied cultures. The Waste Land is, in a more visual sense, bits of history—stories broken up by war and reassemb</span>
  <span id="freeText6194810520046338597" style="display:none">T.S. Eliot’s poem The Waste Land is a collection of fragments. There are 5 parts to the poem and the connection between these parts are noted to be anything but obvious, yet they all hold different layers of voices and obscure elements to the non-traditional reader. The poem as a whole constantly shifts its point of view and is abundantly full of references of classic literature from varied cultures. The Waste Land is, in a more visual sense, bits of history—stories broken up by war and reassembled into a new frame. I mention war, because Eliot witnessed the after effect combat plagues upon a nation from its casualties, for he finished The Waste Land after WWI in 1922. Eliot wrote about what he saw not what he felt. He wrote disassociated from himself and in the form of many other voices because he wanted to cultivate an inclusive human consciousness—an ordered and collective truth. <br /><br />Noted in The Observer, one of Britain’s top new leaders, The Waste Land has been said to be,”…one of the most important poems of the 20th century”. Arguably, this could be true to some, yet very false to others, namely those who have no knowledge of Eliot’s many allusions throughout the work. The poem is also noted to be difficult because of its ever changing scenes and at times various points of view and/or speakers. In one scene you’re a woman having a drink and discussing with your friend about getting dentures then, you are the bartender closing up shop and ushering patrons out. Yet, despite its difficulty to most, the poem has within itself a serious concern: the loss of our shared culture; predominately the classic and the public’s awareness of its life within our time. It’s ultimately important to read and care about this poem because, it is a work of art and a catalyst for one to not forget the past culture that is losing space, not only with time but, in the hearts and minds of us all.<br /><br />A great deal of symbolic ideas came from Jessie Weston’s book, From Ritual to Romance. Eliot himself is quoted saying, “Not only were the title but, the plan and a good deal of the incidental symbolism of the poem suggested by [Weston’s] book. <br /><br />From Ritual to Romance is a work that covers in depth the legend of the holy grail, also using terms such as ‘Fischer King’ and ‘ waste land’, which we can read and see in Eliot’s poem along with notes tarot cards. Eliot first introduces the cards in lines 46-54 with Madam Sosostris and ultimately follows through to the end in lines 427-430, foretelling the physical falling of the London Bridge but, also the fall of a great figure as well. <br /><br />In using such symbols and grand myths, like presented in From Ritual to Romance, it allowed Eliot to suggest that beneath ones everyday streets, like his own London town, there is an old story we all share even though it’s all in pieces below our feet. Eliot connects past stories and myths to suggest our history, as a whole, is intertwined with our present and future life. <br /><br />In the beginning of Eliot’s poem we are met with an epigraph. You may be asking yourself what is an epigraph? Simply, it’s like a quote or introduction for what your about to read.<br />Furthermore, the epigraph Eliot chose is one of a Roman nature written around the 1st century A.D. This ancient manuscript, like The Waste Land was written in fragments, and was titled, “Satyricon” by Petronius.<br /><br />The story tells of an oracle, Sybil, who wished for immortality yet forgot to add the favor of youth in accordance to so many years. So, she forever was cursed rather than blessed to live out the rest of her days getting older and older. What Eliot’s quote describes in the beginning is a conversation with Sybil and a group of boys which ask her if she has anymore wishes, and she replies that she does and that is to die. This epigraph is significant because it’s a foreshadowing for what’s to come as well as an allusion to the spiritual and cultural death of the western world.<br /> <br />Allusions in The Waste Land are like an abstract painting, different voices telling the same story from different angles. Because allusion expands meaning, connects this story with what it historically or literally refers—all those different voices from other poems, essentially allowed Eliot to make this poem significantly larger than itself; because it in essence is all poems. Eliot chose this multi-layered writing, filled with obscure historical events and classic literature, because he had a specific audience in mind. T.S. Eliot’s polished ability to interweave pieces of literature gold within a 430 lined poem is not only genius but a test of sorts for those who pick it up; a test to ultimately see if you can understand Eliot’s mournful view—the loss of our shared culture through ever changing times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6194810520046338597" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2034949979" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2034949979" />
    <a title="Joshua" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63058444-joshua"><img alt="Joshua" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592024737p2/63058444.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2034949979?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshua" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshua" href="/user/show/63058444-joshua">Joshua</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451526847" href="/book/show/118391.The_Waste_Land_and_Other_Poems">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2034949979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8130345136661983850">This collection gathers the most popular of Eliot's poetry together in one slim volume and the reader, depending on the quality of their high English school teachers, is sure to remember many of them. Whether it's Journey of the Magi, The Hollow Men, The Waste Land, or the Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock these poems demonstrate why T.S. Eliot was one of the most important poets of the modern period. Eliot as a writer established a unique voice arranging the words in every poem in such a way that</span>
  <span id="freeText8130345136661983850" style="display:none">This collection gathers the most popular of Eliot's poetry together in one slim volume and the reader, depending on the quality of their high English school teachers, is sure to remember many of them. Whether it's Journey of the Magi, The Hollow Men, The Waste Land, or the Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock these poems demonstrate why T.S. Eliot was one of the most important poets of the modern period. Eliot as a writer established a unique voice arranging the words in every poem in such a way that it spoke directly to the alienation of the reader and let them see inside of their own hearts.<br /><br />For my own reading The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock remains the most powerful poem in the collection for the way it manages to express the pain of wanting to speak, but withholding for fear of being thought a fool.<br /><br />Even if the reader is no fan of poetry this collection should still be considered because Eliot has, despite the time that has passed, managed to remain a writer worth reading and worth remembering.<br /><br />If the reader would like to read my full review of the poem Prufrock they can follow the link below to my site White Tower Musings:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://jsjammersmith.wordpress.com/2016/03/16/coffee-spoons-dignity-bravery-missed-have-you-had-your-prufrock-moment/">https://jsjammersmith.wordpress.com/2...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8130345136661983850" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vincent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vincent" href="/user/show/1129593-vincent">Vincent</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="cloth 9781603110020" href="/book/show/3019029-the-waste-land-prufrock-and-other-observations">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1129593-vincent?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1129593-vincent?shelf=e-read-book">e-read-book</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60148237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6181509378934404303">This volume contains Eliot's first two books of Poetry and his magnum opus, "The Waste Land," with its much needed (and thankfully) introductions, foot notes and critical commentary. Without the afterword analysis, I don't think I would appreciate some of the earlier poems as much as I eventually was able to do. I particularily enjoyed several where I did not feel as lost as to the poet's thematic meaning or narrative. Mr. Eliot's literary aspirations and ideals for great poetry are that they ma</span>
  <span id="freeText6181509378934404303" style="display:none">This volume contains Eliot's first two books of Poetry and his magnum opus, "The Waste Land," with its much needed (and thankfully) introductions, foot notes and critical commentary. Without the afterword analysis, I don't think I would appreciate some of the earlier poems as much as I eventually was able to do. I particularily enjoyed several where I did not feel as lost as to the poet's thematic meaning or narrative. Mr. Eliot's literary aspirations and ideals for great poetry are that they maintain a "comprehensive" vision and many enlightening "allusions," from history, religion and literature the world over. He surely does this in "The Waste Land" and although I enjoyed reading his masterpiece, but finding it very difficult to follow at times, it must be read thoroughly and openly through numerous sittings to understand all the many thematic layers and enjoy a full appreciation and understanding. Appreciation, I can muster now, but a full understanding I may never do, due to the vast knowledge and intelligence of the man. But, I will read it again, and again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6181509378934404303" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4383997824982666018">Please read "The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock." It is my favorite poem and it quite possibly changed my life. Never have I experienced a piece of literature that I have heard interpreted in so many different manners--this, in addition to my personal reading of Prufrock, has led me to believe that everyone can find themself in it on some level. Moreover, everytime I read it, I pick up a new piece of something...a glimmering something that had slipped past me times before. I am in love with thi</span>
  <span id="freeText4383997824982666018" style="display:none">Please read "The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock." It is my favorite poem and it quite possibly changed my life. Never have I experienced a piece of literature that I have heard interpreted in so many different manners--this, in addition to my personal reading of Prufrock, has led me to believe that everyone can find themself in it on some level. Moreover, everytime I read it, I pick up a new piece of something...a glimmering something that had slipped past me times before. I am in love with this poem. It is an anthem for artists of every medium--the struggle of the everyman who is searching for his place in the world--the hipster's conundrum: how do I wear my pants to make me look like I don't care? Or do I care? This poem epitomizes the essence of the existential crisis. </span>

<div id="review_3325140571" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3325140571" />
    <a title="Brandon R. Skanes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65297771-brandon-r-skanes"><img alt="Brandon R. Skanes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604795257p2/65297771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3325140571?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brandon R. Skanes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandon R. Skanes" href="/user/show/65297771-brandon-r-skanes">Brandon R. Skanes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3325140571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17702014454228486213">As a man with an English degree, I knew I would love this collection of poems. To be honest, this is actually my first time sitting down and reading the poems of T.S. Eliot. Keeping that in mind, a lot of the works in this collection focus on the "human condition" which is a primary aspect of modernism. Along with Beckett, writers such as Eliot in the 1920s and 1930s became a large influence for writers like Isaac Asimov, who is known for writing the first science fiction novella in, 1947, I bel</span>
  <span id="freeText17702014454228486213" style="display:none">As a man with an English degree, I knew I would love this collection of poems. To be honest, this is actually my first time sitting down and reading the poems of T.S. Eliot. Keeping that in mind, a lot of the works in this collection focus on the "human condition" which is a primary aspect of modernism. Along with Beckett, writers such as Eliot in the 1920s and 1930s became a large influence for writers like Isaac Asimov, who is known for writing the first science fiction novella in, 1947, I believe? Anyway, it is clear to me that poems such as "The Wasteland" depicting the human mind, as, well a wasteland, become a key factor for the popularity of science fiction books in the 1950s onward. A final thought, I realize that writers such as Asimov and Eliot were a large inspiration for the "Fallout" video game franchise, which is a series I love. So, I really enjoyed this collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17702014454228486213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

These poems read in his voice are perfect for a rainy day. The melancholy Prufrock, painful Wasteland and Quartets finished by the whimsical Old Possum’s Cats combine to display the incredible range of T S Eliot’s mastery of mood and language. Thoroughly enjoyable. </span>

<div id="review_2019921402" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2019921402" />
    <a title="Andrew Barger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3788369-andrew-barger"><img alt="Andrew Barger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442487029p2/3788369.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2019921402?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew Barger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Barger" href="/user/show/3788369-andrew-barger">Andrew Barger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2019921402" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14484506266582971522">The routine and regimented ways in which to write a T. S. Eliot poem in 10 easy steps are as follows, ahem:<br/><br/>1. Come upon a cute turn of phrase,<br/>2. Pen a rambunctious title loosely related to said turn of phrase,<br/>3. Thumb the pages of Dante and pluck out an epigraph (preferably in the original Italian),<br/>4. Insert said epigraph beneath said title,<br/>5. Write slapdash quatrains,<br/>6. Insert said turn of phrase at a random place in said quatrains,<br/>7. Run the occasional line of said quatrains onto the next line</span>
  <span id="freeText14484506266582971522" style="display:none">The routine and regimented ways in which to write a T. S. Eliot poem in 10 easy steps are as follows, ahem:<br /><br />1. Come upon a cute turn of phrase,<br />2. Pen a rambunctious title loosely related to said turn of phrase,<br />3. Thumb the pages of Dante and pluck out an epigraph (preferably in the original Italian),<br />4. Insert said epigraph beneath said title,<br />5. Write slapdash quatrains,<br />6. Insert said turn of phrase at a random place in said quatrains,<br />7. Run the occasional line of said quatrains onto the next line,<br />8. Allude to a Bible verse (preferably the Old Testament),<br />9. Add italics to random words, and<br />10. Tell short tales within the poem that have no connection to the others.<br /><br />Eliot is said to have ushered in the modernist movement in poetry. That is to say he largely did away with rhyming and sentimentality employed by the Victorians. He painted a dying world in the relatively few poems he gave us. "The Waste Land" is considered his best--perhaps because it is his longest. "The Hollow Men," however, shines brightest with its Shakespearean and Heart of Darkness references. A number of songs have been written about Eliot's poems and the famous play Cats was based on his Old Possum's Book of Practical Cats.<br /><br />"The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock" "Afternoons and Coffeespoons" Crash Test Dummies God Shuffled His Feet 1993 <br />"The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock" "Creep" Radiohead Pablo Honey 1993<br />"The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock" “Peaches” The Presidents of the United States of America Presidents of the United States of America 1995<br />"The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock" “The Message” Grand Master Flash The Message 1982<br />"The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock" "Thick as a Brick" Jethro Tull Thick as a Brick 1972<br />Old Possum’s Book of Practical Cats Cats Musical Andrew Lloyd Webber<br />"The Wasteland" "The First Day of Spring" Noah and the Whale<br />"The Wasteland" “Wasteland” Dan Bern Dan Bern 1993<br /><br />Meh is how I would sum up the body of poetry Eliot left us. It certainly pails in scope and impact to the poems of Edgar Allan Poe. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6990156.Edgar_Allan_Poe_s_Annotated_Poems" title="Edgar Allan Poe's Annotated Poems by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">Edgar Allan Poe's Annotated Poems</a> One has to wonder if he would be known at all without the litter of jellicle cat poems he penned, had more than nine lives on the West End and Broadway.<br /><br />#tseliot #reviewoftseliot</span>
  <a data-text-id="14484506266582971522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2015691156" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2015691156" />
    <a title="Patyta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64441785-patyta"><img alt="Patyta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510380595p2/64441785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2015691156?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patyta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patyta" href="/user/show/64441785-patyta">Patyta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451526847" href="/book/show/118391.The_Waste_Land_and_Other_Poems">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64441785-patyta?shelf=poes%C3%ADa">poesía</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2015691156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11803989421637358714"><blockquote><b>[...]<br/> At the violet hour, when the eyes and back<br/>Turn upward from the desk, when the human engine waits<br/><br/>Like a taxi throbbing waiting,<br/>I Tiresias, though blind, throbbing between two lives,<br/><br/>Old man with wrinkled female breasts, can see<br/>At the violet hour, the evening hour that strives<br/>Homeward, and brings the sailor home from sea,<br/>The typist home at tea-time, clears her breakfast, lights<br/><br/>Her stove, and lays out food in tins.<br/>Out of the window perilously spread<br/>Her drying combinations touched by the sun’s </b></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11803989421637358714" style="display:none"><blockquote><b>[...]<br /> At the violet hour, when the eyes and back<br />Turn upward from the desk, when the human engine waits<br /><br />Like a taxi throbbing waiting,<br />I Tiresias, though blind, throbbing between two lives,<br /><br />Old man with wrinkled female breasts, can see<br />At the violet hour, the evening hour that strives<br />Homeward, and brings the sailor home from sea,<br />The typist home at tea-time, clears her breakfast, lights<br /><br />Her stove, and lays out food in tins.<br />Out of the window perilously spread<br />Her drying combinations touched by the sun’s last rays,<br /><br />On the divan are piled (at night her bed)<br />Stockings, slippers, camisoles, and stays.<br />I Tiresias, old man with wrinkled dugs<br />Perceived the scene, and foretold the rest—<br />I too awaited the expected guest.<br />He, the young man carbuncular, arrives,<br />A small house-agent’s clerk, with one bold stare,<br />One of the low on whom assurance sits<br />As a silk hat on a Bradford millionaire.<br />The time is now propitious, as he guesses,<br />The meal is ended, she is bored and tired,<br />Endeavours to engage her in caresses<br />Which still are unreproved, if undesired.<br />Flushed and decided, he assaults at once;<br />Exploring hands encounter no defence;<br />His vanity requires no response,<br />And makes a welcome of indifference.<br />(And I Tiresias have foresuffered all<br />Enacted on this same divan or bed;<br />I who have sat by Thebes below the wall<br />And walked among the lowest of the dead.)<br />Bestows one final patronizing kiss,<br />And gropes his way, finding the stairs unlit…<br /> <br />She turns and looks a moment in the glass,<br />Hardly aware of her departed lover;	<br />Her brain allows one half-formed thought to pass:<br />“Well now that’s done: and I’m glad it’s over.”<br />When lovely woman stoops to folly and<br />Paces about her room again, alone,<br />She smoothes her hair with automatic hand,<br />And puts a record on the gramophone.[...]</b><br /><i>The Waste Land,</i> III, The Fire Sermon</blockquote><br /><br />En no más de dos días, descartando el sueño de las noches, pasé de saber apenas quién era el señor Eliot, autor de la celebérrima <i>The Waste Land</i> que sin embargo jamás había leído, a una irremediable fan. Y no es que me tomara esos dos días el quedarme prendada a sus palabras, enamorarme perdida de su versos, pues eso, el amor a primera vista, sucedió apenas iniciando el libro, al leer y luego releer y rereleer una y otra vez “The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock”, la más melancólicamente optimista visión del moderno hombre del siglo XX... o quizás XXI (¡el poema fue publicado hace un siglo, 1917!)<br /><br /><blockquote><b>Let us go then, you and I, <br />When the evening is spread out against the sky <br />Like a patient etherized upon a table; <br />Let us go, through certain half-deserted streets, <br />The muttering retreats <br />Of restless nights in one-night cheap hotels <br />And sawdust restaurants with oyster-shells: <br />Streets that follow like a tedious argument <br />Of insidious intent <br />To lead you to an overwhelming question ... <br />Oh, do not ask, “What is it?” <br />Let us go and make our visit. <br /><br />In the room the women come and go <br />Talking of Michelangelo. <br /><br />The yellow fog that rubs its back upon the window-panes, <br />The yellow smoke that rubs its muzzle on the window-panes, <br />Licked its tongue into the corners of the evening, <br />Lingered upon the pools that stand in drains, <br />Let fall upon its back the soot that falls from chimneys, <br />Slipped by the terrace, made a sudden leap, <br />And seeing that it was a soft October night, <br />Curled once about the house, and fell asleep. <br /><br />And indeed there will be time <br />For the yellow smoke that slides along the street, <br />Rubbing its back upon the window-panes; <br />There will be time, there will be time <br />To prepare a face to meet the faces that you meet; <br />There will be time to murder and create, <br />And time for all the works and days of hands <br />That lift and drop a question on your plate; <br />Time for you and time for me, <br />And time yet for a hundred indecisions, <br />And for a hundred visions and revisions, <br />Before the taking of a toast and tea.[...]</b><br />“The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock”</blockquote><br /><br />Este pequeño libro, nada más que una muestra representativa de lo mejor de sus primeros poemas, aquellos que (según decir de la editora) revolucionaron la lírica inglesa, es un auténtico portento, versos que me calaron hondo y en ellos me extravié durante horas, y si bien es innegable que, hoy día, acostumbradísimos a ver retratada de tal “real” forma la vida cotidiana, el impacto no podría ser el mismo que seguro sintieron los primeros lectores de Eliot, el retrato que éste hace del transcurrir de nuestras horas sigue siendo no obstante asombroso.<br /><br />Y no sé, todavía no sé, pero pronto lo averiguaré, supongo, qué tanto la visión y arte de Eliot cambiaron con el paso de los años, asunto mencionado en la introducción al libro, pero aquí, al menos, esa modernidad tiene aún visos de esperanza, puede percibirse una pequeña luz al final del largo túnel, pues no sólo trabajamos, comemos, nos bañamos, compramos en centros comerciales y viajamos en gusanos metálicos bajo tierra: también soñamos, anhelamos, deseamos y, en el aislamiento y fragmentación del espacio citadino, nuestras vidas siguen conectadas al gran todo del mundo.<br /><br />Y es que, en líneas muy generales, de eso es de lo que nos hablan <i>The Waste Land</i>, Sweeney, “The Hollow Men”, o “La figlia che piange”, una inmensidad de la que todos somos parte, y que aún apunta hacia adelante, presagiando o entreviendo o anhelando un descubrimiento.<br /><br />Revelación que sin embargo no se produce, y que, con el horror de la Gran Guerra, da paso a las dudas, a un atolondramiento, cuestionamiento y desánimo, perfectamente reflejados en <i>Ash Wednesday</i> y “The Journey of the Magi”, en que la voz del poeta respira su pesar y desconsuelo, ya mitad arrepentido de haber abandonado sus referentes, extraviado, aislado y fragmentado sin contrapeso alguno.<br /><br /><blockquote><b>[...]<br /> All this was a long time ago, I remember,<br />And I would do it again, but set down<br />This set down<br />This: were we led all that way for<br />Birth or Death? There was a Birth, certainly<br />We had evidence and no doubt. I had seen birth and death,<br /><br />But had thought they were different; this Birth was<br />Hard and bitter agony for us, like Death, our death.<br />We returned to our places, these Kingdoms,<br />But no longer at ease here, in the old dispensation,<br />With an alien people clutching their gods.<br />I should be glad of another death.</b><br />“The Journey of the Magi”</blockquote><br /><br />Con un ritmo y musicalidad maravillosas, este primer Eliot es quizá lo mejor que haya leído en lo que va del año... y ya son muchos los libros.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11803989421637358714" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_481005312" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/481005312" />
    <a title="George Tyson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15204969-george-tyson"><img alt="George Tyson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442517030p2/15204969.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/481005312?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George Tyson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George Tyson" href="/user/show/15204969-george-tyson">George Tyson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15204969-george-tyson?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer481005312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11035129747402924497">Right - like I'm really going to critique the works of T.S. Eliot. However, I will say this: because some of his poetry contains bits and pieces from other works of literature that span Western and Eastern poetry, fiction, philosophy and theology, it's great fun to deconstruct those poems to see how they were "put together." (For example, see <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://eliotswasteland.tripod.com/'>http://eliotswasteland.tripod.com/</a>) However, to really appreciate what Elliot was getting at, you need to reassemble these fragments. That's because the ma</span>
  <span id="freeText11035129747402924497" style="display:none">Right - like I'm really going to critique the works of T.S. Eliot. However, I will say this: because some of his poetry contains bits and pieces from other works of literature that span Western and Eastern poetry, fiction, philosophy and theology, it's great fun to deconstruct those poems to see how they were "put together." (For example, see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://eliotswasteland.tripod.com/">http://eliotswasteland.tripod.com/</a>) However, to really appreciate what Elliot was getting at, you need to reassemble these fragments. That's because the manner in which he juxtaposes them transforms his poems from dazzling displays of craft and literacy into some of the most eloquent statements of twentieth century existentialism and the anguish that it can entail. (In his later poems he seems to retreat from the "desert" of existentialism into the shade of the Christian faith. This is most notable in "Ash Wednesday" which marked his conversion to Catholicism in the latter part of his life.)<br /><br />I might add that I first read The Wasteland and Prufrock in 9th grade and hardly understood a word of them. And yet they struck a chord that's made me return to them ever since. Back then, I regarded them as an elegant "code" that was beyond me to decipher. But as I've gotten older, I've cracked it in my own way. To me at least, they contain an urgent warning that our culture is slowly collapsing under the weight of human nature and the lack of a convincing argument that human life has intrinsic meaning. I hope I'm not slandering Mr. Eliot by interpreting him that way. I also hope that he's mistaken, if that's really what he meant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11035129747402924497" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_79548685" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/79548685" />
    <a title="Lisa Litberg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/264999-lisa-litberg"><img alt="Lisa Litberg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442980399p2/264999.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79548685?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa Litberg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa Litberg" href="/user/show/264999-lisa-litberg">Lisa Litberg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="cloth 9781603110020" href="/book/show/3019029-the-waste-land-prufrock-and-other-observations">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79548685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4674669958127836097"><i>"Let us go then, you and I, when the evening is spread out against the sky like a patient etherized upon a table..."</i><br/><br/>This collection contains two of Eliot's most famous works, The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock and The Waste Land, but it also includes all the other poems he published betwen 1917 and 1922. I love Eliot and his use of allusion and symbolism in poetry. Because this volume is annotated there was a constant string of footnotes translating text and explaining references in the poems, </span>
  <span id="freeText4674669958127836097" style="display:none"><i>"Let us go then, you and I, when the evening is spread out against the sky like a patient etherized upon a table..."</i><br /><br />This collection contains two of Eliot's most famous works, The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock and The Waste Land, but it also includes all the other poems he published betwen 1917 and 1922. I love Eliot and his use of allusion and symbolism in poetry. Because this volume is annotated there was a constant string of footnotes translating text and explaining references in the poems, which I often found more distracting than helpful, but which certainly does come in handy when you're trying to make sense of poetry such as this. But really, do we have to make sense of it? Can't we just read it and let it sift through our own experiences, connect with our own history? I so enjoy reading phrases like "The worlds revolve like ancient women/gathering fuel in vacant lots" or "In this decayed hole among the mountains/in the faint moonlight, the grass is singing/over the tumbled graves, about the chapel/There is the empty chapel, only the wind's home./It has no windows, and the door swings,/Dry bones can harm no one."<br /><br />Eliot has other works that are not contained in this volume that are worth reading as well, so I wouldn't recommend this as a definitive sample of Eliot, but rather an introduction with much explanation as to how to approach Eliot's poetry. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4674669958127836097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('OXKAixbHjhuJVWvFkXD/RCK9gBzC0WM719/73dv8O//WSqeTKJ9GrnWUq08Wqpz65idmmCUZ2nkErtVSOTd5Hw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0+FFt42PIM0jdgxEfEZrqJZgxKXvSFKi0Db9DBbY44Q82WKvs9foeN+3zM77nAgWUvoiIQiA6+ADR9OD9BOhZA==')})

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/376741.Prelude"><img alt="Prelude" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1328848456l/376741._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_376741'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/376741.Prelude?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Prelude<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/45712.Katherine_Mansfield?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Katherine Mansfield<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.84 avg rating &mdash; 285 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1918\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer15616868682316171852\">One of Katherine Mansfield\'s most important works, Prelude established her reputation as a master of short fiction. It is accompanied here by its continuation pieces At the Bay and The Doll\'s House. A large, seemingly loving family, the Burnells lead<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15616868682316171852\" style=\"display:none\">One of Katherine Mansfield\'s most important works, Prelude established her reputation as a master of short fiction. It is accompanied here by its continuation pieces At the Bay and The Doll\'s House. A large, seemingly loving family, the Burnells lead an idyllic life, with the children free to play at will, and with enough adults on hand to lavish love upon them. Yet alongside this innocent evocation of childhood bliss, another aspect of the household is delicately and unmistakably laid bare as Mansfield exposes the true nature of the family. Pretensions and insecurities rise to the surface in a series of vivid, all too real impressions of domestic life. The result is an exquisite rendering of childhood, memory, and understanding. A key figure in the Modernist movement, Katherine Mansfield is most remarkable for having perfected the art of the short story.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15616868682316171852\" href=\"#\" In America she has been posthumously launched as the electric-age Blake, she has been translated into French and Italian to great acclaim, and in the Times Literary Supplement Thom Gunn compared her to the great Augustan satirists. Her reclamation as an English poet is long overdue.\n\nPound, Moore and Williams valued her work, while British critics openly scorned it. Not only were her futurist techniques unlike anything they had encountered before, but her subjects -- procreation, parturition, prostitution, suicide, addiction, retardation -- were considered shocking even by some modernists.\n\nShe vanished from the literary scene just as dramatically as she had arrived on it, and for much of the century her bold experiments remained a well-kept secret. Carcanet first introduced her work to British readers in 1985 in Roger Conover\'s The Last Lunar Baedeker, a collected writings. This new edition updates our earlier volume and presents more reliable texts of the essential Loy poems. It includes more extensive notes and apparatus, and features a number of previously unknown works rescued from Dada archives and obscure avant-garde little magazines. All of Loy\'s canonical Futurist and feminist satires are included, as are the celebrated poems from her Paris and New York periods, the complete cycle of \`Love Songs\', and her famous portraits-in-verse which define the trajectory of her favoured company and geography -- from fellow modernists Joyce and Brancusi in Paris in the 1920s to fellow destitutes in New York\'s Lower East Saxberger has written nothing for thirty years, yet he now realises that he is more than merely an Unremarkable Civil Servant, after all: a Venerable Poet, for whom Late Fame is inevitable – if, that is, his new acolytes are to be believed…Arthur Schnitzler was one of the most admired, provocative European writers of the twentieth century. The Nazis attempted to burn all of his work, but his archive was miraculously saved, and with it, Late Fame. Never published before, it is a treasure, a perfect satire of literary self-regard and charlatanism.Arthur Schnitzler (b. 1862 in Vienna) was one of the most influential European writers of the twentieth century, perhaps best known here for his novellas Dream Story and Fräulein Else. He qualified as a doctor but was increasingly driven to a career in writing, resulting in several celebrated plays, novellas and novels which explore the great existential subjects of the modern age: relationships, love, sex, ageing and death. Because his work dealt with subjects considered taboo, he frequently attracted the hostility of the authorities, consequently losing his position as Chief Medic in the Reserve Army and being tried for disorderly conduct. Schnitzler was close friends with Stefan Zweig and Sigmund Freud, who both admired him greatly, and a member of the \'Young Vienna\' circle of writers who regularly met at a café nicknamed \'Café Megalomania\' - the very same clique and café he satirises so deliciously in Late Fame. Schnitzler died in 1931.Pushkin Press also publishes his novellas Fräulein Else, Dying and Casanova\'s Return to Venice.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17520543873914951887\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/18540.T_S_Eliot">About T.S. Eliot</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/18540.T_S_Eliot"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1507887310p3/18540.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/18540.T_S_Eliot">T.S. Eliot
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,975
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4524425018044177574"><b>Thomas Stearns Eliot</b> was a poet, dramatist and literary critic. He received the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1948 "for his outstanding, pioneer contribution to present-day poetry." He wrote the poems <i>The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock</i>, <i>The Waste Land</i>, <i>The Hollow Men</i>, <i>Ash Wednesday</i>, and <i>Four Quartets</i>; the plays <i>Murder in the Cathedral</i> and <i>The Cocktail Party</i>; and the essay <i>Tradition and the Individ</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4524425018044177574" style="display:none"><b>Thomas Stearns Eliot</b> was a poet, dramatist and literary critic. He received the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1948 "for his outstanding, pioneer contribution to present-day poetry." He wrote the poems <i>The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock</i>, <i>The Waste Land</i>, <i>The Hollow Men</i>, <i>Ash Wednesday</i>, and <i>Four Quartets</i>; the plays <i>Murder in the Cathedral</i> and <i>The Cocktail Party</i>; and the essay <i>Tradition and the Individual Talent</i>. Eliot was born an American, moved to the United Kingdom in 1914 (at the age of 25), and became a British subject in 1927 at the age of 39.<br /><br />See also <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T.S._Eliot">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T.S._Eliot</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4524425018044177574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
